The Disliked Act of Playing in Prayer
Ruling on playing in prayer with two proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on playing during prayer and identify the two proofs (hadith and qiyas) that support it.

Core Idea
Playing during prayer is makruh tanzih, not invalidating, even though the same act outside prayer is haram.

Many worshippers wonder about minor movements during prayer—adjusting clothes, scratching, or fidgeting. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position by defining the term 'abath (عبث) and presenting both a prophetic hadith and a rational analogy. Understanding this distinction helps avoid unnecessary worry while maintaining reverence in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The source text states that playing with one's clothes or body during prayer is makruh (disliked). Note the exact wording: 'مکروہ' (makruh), which in Hanafi fiqh generally means makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) unless specified otherwise.
2
Locate the primary dalil (hadith)
The author cites a hadith where the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said Allah has made three things disliked, one being playing during prayer. Identify this as a sunnah ahad proof, which is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah, hence the ruling is not at the level of haram or fard.
The exact quote in the text is a paraphrase; the full hadith is known in various collections.
3
Understand the secondary dalil (qiyas)
The second proof is a rational argument: an act of 'abath (pointless worldly action) is haram outside prayer, so it should also be haram in prayer. However, the Hanafi school overrides this qiyas with the hadith, concluding that it is only makruh in prayer due to the special sanctity and other considerations.
This shows how a weaker qiyas can be limited by a stronger textual evidence.
4
Categorize the hukm level
The ruling is makruh tanzih: the prayer is valid, but the act is blameworthy and should be avoided. If a person does it, the prayer is still accepted, but they lose reward for reverence.
Hanafis distinguish between makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Here it is tanzih.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider excessive playful movements as invalidating prayer if they are three consecutive actions. Hanafis restrict this to only if the movement is 'amal kathir (many actions) that changes the posture significantly.
This highlights the practical impact of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلوة وما يكره فيها. تشریح: ماسبق میں مفسدات نماز کا بیان تھا، اس فصل میں مکروہات کا ذکر ہے۔ امام بدر الدین کر دری کے قول کے مطابق عبث وہ ہے جس میں غرض تو ہو مگر شرعی نہ ہو، اور سفہ وہ ہے جس میں کوئی غرض نہ ہو۔ مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ نمازی کا اپنے کپڑے یا بدن سے کھیلنا مکروہ ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے تمہارے لئے تین چیزیں مکروہ کی ہیں، ان میں سے ایک نماز کے اندر کھیلنا ہے، اور باقی دو میں سے ایک روزہ کی حالت میں گندی گفتگو کرنا ہے، اور دوسری قبرستان میں قہقہہ لگانا ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ فعل عبث نماز سے باہر حرام ہے، پس نماز میں بھی حرام ہے۔
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. Explanation: The previous discussion was about invalidators of prayer; this chapter mentions the disliked acts. According to Imam Badr al-Din al-Kardari, 'abath' is that which has a purpose but not a religious one, and 'safah' is that which has no purpose at all. The ruling is that it is makruh for the worshipper to play with his clothes or body. The evidence is that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said: Allah has made three things disliked for you: one is playing during prayer, the second is foul talk while fasting, and the third is laughing at graves. The second evidence is that an act of 'abath is haram outside prayer, so it is also haram in prayer.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage opens the section on disliked acts in prayer. It first defines two key terms: 'abath (عبث) which is an act with some worldly purpose but no religious benefit, and 'safah (سفہ) which is a completely purposeless act. The author then states the ruling: any playing with one's clothes or body during prayer is makruh (disliked). Two proofs are given: first, a hadith where the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم listed three things Allah has made disliked, including playing in prayer; second, a rational argument (qiyas) that if such behavior is haram outside prayer, it is even more inappropriate during prayer. The ruling is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) unless the act is excessive. This is based on ahad hadith, so the level of obligation is not as strong as a mutawatir proof. The chapter title 'ما يفسد الصلوة وما يكره فيها' indicates it covers both invalidators (which were in earlier chapters) and disliked acts (this chapter).

Key Terms
عبث ('abath): An act done with some worldly purpose but no religious benefit.
سفہ (safah): An act with absolutely no purpose, neither worldly nor religious.
مکروہ (makruh): Disliked; an act that is not sinful but is discouraged; leaving it is rewarded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a person who plays with his beard or clothes during prayer according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) It invalidates the prayer (bāṭil)
B) It is haram and the prayer must be repeated
C) It is makruh (disliked) but the prayer is valid
D) It is permissible (mubah)

Key Takeaway
Playing in prayer is makruh tanzih, not haram or invalid, based on the explicit hadith and the overriding of the qiyas argument.

Turning Pebbles in Prayer: The Ruling and Its Proof
Ruling on smoothing pebbles at the place of prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on smoothing pebbles during prayer and identify the evidence (hadith and rational) that supports the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Smoothing pebbles in prayer is makruh tanzih unless needed; then one motion only is permitted.

In this lesson, we examine the specific act of levelling the prostration spot during salah. While unnecessary movement is discouraged as 'abath, a genuine need—such as a pebble preventing proper sajdah—permits a single smoothing motion. We will explore the prophetic evidence from Abu Dharr and Mu'ayqib, and the rational basis of prayer rectification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is turning/smoothing pebbles during prayer. The default ruling is makruh tanzih because it is considered 'abath (purposeless activity).
2
Note the exception
If one cannot prostrate properly due to pebbles, levelling the spot once is allowed. This exception is based on necessity.
The need must be genuine; otherwise the default rule applies.
3
Examine the textual proof
The hadith of Abu Dharr and Mu'ayqib explicitly permits only one wipe: 'once, or leave it.' This limits the action to a single motion.
The wording 'واحدة' is decisive.
4
Consider the rational proof
Smoothing pebbles rectifies the prayer itself, so it is not objectionable. This rational argument (maslaha) supports the exception but does not override the textual limit.
The rational proof explains the 'illah (cause) for permissibility.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance
In ghayr Zahir al-Riwayah, two motions are allowed, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from Zahir al-Riwayah is one.
This shows the hierarchy of authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقلب الحصا لانه نوع عبث الا ان لا يمكنه من السجود فيسويه مرة لقوله عليه السلام مرة يا اباذر ولان فيه اصلاح صلاته. تشریح: مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ نماز کی حالت میں کنکریاں نہ لوٹے اس لئے کہ یہ بھی ایک طرح کا فعل عبث ہے۔ ہاں اگر سجدہ کرنا ممکن نہ ہو تو ایک بار موضع سجدہ کو برابر کر سکتا ہے۔ غیر ظاہر الروایہ میں دو مرتبہ کی بھی اجازت ہے۔ دلیل حضور کا ترجمہ یا اباذر والا فذر ہے یعنی اے ابوذر ایک بار ور نہ اس کو بھی چھوڑ مراد یہ ہے کہ موضع سجدہ سے ایک بار کنکریاں ہٹانے کی اجازت اور اگر ایک بار بھی نہ ہٹائے بلکہ چھوڑ دے تو یہ افضل ہے۔ علامہ ابن الہمام شارح ہدایہ نے یہ لکھا ہے کہ حدیث ان الفاظ کے ساتھ غریب ہے عبدالرزاق نے حضرت ابوذر رضی اللہ عنہ سے یہ حدیث ان الفاظ کے ساتھ نقل کی ہے سئلت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن كل شئى حتى عن مسح الحصا - واحدة اودع حضرت ابوذر کہتے ہیں کہ میں نے حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم سے ہر چیز کے بارے میں سوال کیا حتی کہ کنکریوں کو ہٹانے کے بارے میں بھی تو آپ نے فرمایا کہ ایک بارور نہ چھوڑ دے۔ اور معیقیب سے روایت ہے کہ انه صلى الله عليه وسلم قال تمسح الحصا وانت تصلى فان كنت لا بد فاعلا فواحدۃ یعنی حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا کہ کنکریاں مت بناؤ درانحالیکہ تم نماز میں ہو پس اگر ضروری کرنا پڑ جائے تو ایک بار۔ عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کنکریاں ہٹانے میں اپنی نماز کی اصلاح ہے اور جس عمل سے نماز کی اصلاح مقصود ہو اس میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں۔
And he should not turn pebbles because it is a type of 'abath, unless he cannot prostrate, then he may level it once, due to his saying عليه السلام: 'once, O Abu Dharr' and because there is in it the rectification of his prayer. Explanation: The ruling is that he should not turn pebbles during prayer because it is also a form of 'abath. However, if he cannot prostrate, he may level the place of prostration once. In non-Zahir al-Riwayah, even twice is permitted. The evidence is the Prophet's words to Abu Dharr: 'once, O Abu Dharr, or leave it.' The meaning is that removing pebbles from the place of prostration once is permitted, and if he does not remove them at all, that is better. Allama Ibn al-Humam, the commentator of al-Hidayah, wrote that this hadith with these words is ghareeb. Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Abu Dharr may Allah be pleased with him: 'I asked the Prophet about everything, even about wiping pebbles; he said: once, or leave it.' And from Mu'ayqib: 'He صلى الله عليه وسلم said: Do not wipe pebbles while you are praying, but if you must, then once.' Rational evidence: Removing pebbles rectifies one's prayer, and an act intended to rectify prayer is not objectionable.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage addresses the specific act of turning or smoothing pebbles at the place of prostration during prayer. The general ruling is that it is makruh because it is a form of 'abath (purposeless activity). However, there is an exception: if the pebbles prevent proper prostration, the worshipper may level them once. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet instructed Abu Dharr to do it only once or leave it. A second hadith from Mu'ayqib echoes this: if you must, then only once. The rational argument is that this act rectifies the prayer itself, so it is not blameworthy. An important intra-Hanafi detail is mentioned: in ghayr Zahir al-Riwayah (non-core transmissions), even two motions are permitted, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is only once. The commentator Ibn al-Humam notes that the hadith wording is gharib (uncommon in transmission). The preferred action is to leave the pebbles untouched entirely.

Key Terms
غیر ظاہر الروایہ (ghayr Zahir al-Riwayah): Transmissions that are not part of the six core books of Imam Muhammad; they carry less authority.
عبث ('abath): An act with no religious benefit; here it refers to unnecessary movement in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, how many times may a person smooth the pebbles at the place of prostration if needed?
A) Not at all
B) Once
C) Twice
D) Three times

Key Takeaway
Smoothing pebbles during prayer is permitted once if needed; otherwise it is makruh tanzih.

The Ruling on Cracking Fingers in Prayer
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cracking fingers in prayer and explain the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Cracking fingers in prayer is makruh, not haram — a prohibition on a non-essential act.

Cracking or snapping one's fingers during prayer is a common habit that may seem trivial, but in Hanafi fiqh it carries a specific ruling. This passage from Ashraf ul Hadaya clarifies that it is makruh (disliked) based on a prophetic hadith. Some scholars extend the dislike to outside prayer. This lesson examines the evidence and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is cracking or snapping the fingers (farqa'at al-asabi'). The ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) during prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is a hadith from Ali ibn Abi Talib: the Prophet said, 'Do not crack your fingers while you are praying.' Another narration adds, 'I like for you what I like for myself.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (nahi) is expressed in the imperative 'la tufarqi' ' (do not crack). In Hanafi usul, a prohibition on a non-essential act during prayer indicates karahah, not tahrim, because the act is external to the prayer's validity.
The hadith is zanni in both thubut and dalala, which further supports karahah over haram.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars extend the karahah to outside prayer because it resembles the act of the people of Lut (Lot). This is an additional opinion, but the primary ruling is limited to prayer.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) during prayer. The prohibition is a guidance to maintain reverence, not a nullifier of the prayer.
If done absent-mindedly, there is no sin, but intentional avoidance is best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يفرقع اصابعه لقوله عليه السلام لاتفرقع اصابعك وانت تصلى. تشریح: نماز کے اندر انگلیوں کا چٹخانا بھی مکروہ ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے حضرت علی رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہ سے فرمایا تھا: إني أحب لك ما أحب لنفسي، لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت تصلي۔ بعض کے نزدیک خارج نماز بھی مکروہ ہے، وجہ کراہت یہ ہے کہ یہ قوم لوط کا فعل ہے۔
And he should not crack his fingers because of his saying عليه السلام: Do not crack your fingers while you are praying. Explanation: Cracking the fingers during prayer is also makruh. The evidence is that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said to Ali may Allah be pleased with him: 'I like for you what I like for myself: do not crack your fingers while you are praying.' According to some, it is also makruh outside prayer, because it is an act of the people of Lut.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on cracking or snapping the fingers (فرقعة الأصابع) during prayer. The ruling is that it is makruh (disliked). Two hadith proofs are cited: first, a general hadith forbidding it during prayer; second, a specific hadith addressed to Ali, emphasizing the Prophet's personal dislike. The text also notes that some scholars extend the karahah to outside prayer because it resembles the practice of the people of Lut (Lot). In Hanafi fiqh, this is considered makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) during prayer; outside prayer, it is also disliked but less so. The reasoning is that it involves unnecessary noise and distraction from the solemnity of prayer.

Key Terms
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa'at al-asabi'): Cracking or snapping the knuckles and finger joints, producing a sound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on cracking fingers during prayer?
A) Haram and invalidates prayer
B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Sunnah (recommended)

Key Takeaway
Cracking fingers in prayer is makruh tanzih based on a prophetic hadith. The prohibition reflects reverence for prayer, and the act should be avoided.

Placing Hands on Hips in Prayer: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on the act of placing hands on hips (takhassur) in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on placing hands on hips (takhassur) during prayer in Hanafi fiqh, cite the primary proof (hadith), and distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzih.

Core Idea
The Prophet explicitly forbade placing hands on hips in prayer, making it makruh tahrimi.

In this lesson, we examine the act of takhassur—placing hands on the hips during prayer. The Hidayah rules it as prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) based on a sound hadith from Abu Hurairah. You will learn the evidence, the rational argument, and how this ruling differs outside of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, note that the act is 'placing hands on the hips' (takhassur) during prayer. The text rules it as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
Makruh tahrimi is a Hanafi category meaning the doer is blameworthy and should avoid it.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The primary evidence is a hadith: the Prophet forbade 'takhassur' in prayer, as narrated by Abu Hurairah. The Arabic text is: 'نهى عن الاختصار في الصلاة'.
This is an ahad hadith, so its thubut (transmission) is zanni, but it is still binding.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The word 'نهى' (forbade) indicates a prohibition. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition (nahy) implies karahah tahrimiyyah unless there is evidence for tahrim (absolute forbiddance).
Thus the ruling is makruh tahrimi, not haram, because the evidence is zanni.
4
Understand the rational proof
The text adds a rational argument: placing hands on hips abandons the sunnah posture of prayer (the preferred hand position). This reinforces the prohibition.
Even without the hadith, the departure from sunnah would make it disliked.
5
Differentiate between inside and outside prayer
During prayer it is makruh tahrimi; outside prayer it is only makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) for both men and women.
This difference shows the heightened adab during salah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتخصر وهو وضع اليدين على الخاصرة لانه عليه السلام نهى عن الاختصار في الصلوة ولان فيه ترك الوضع المسنون. تشریح: نماز کی حالت میں تخصر بھی مکروہ تحریمی ہے کیونکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے نماز میں تخصر کرنے سے منع فرمایا ہے، چنانچہ ابو ہریرہ نے روایت کیا: انه صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الاختصار في الصلاة. عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ تخصر کرنے کی صورت میں مسنون طریقہ کو چھوڑنا لازم آتا ہے۔ خارج صلوۃ مرد اور عورت دونوں کے لئے مکروہ تنزیہی ہے۔ تخصر کی ایک تفسیر تو صاحب ہدایہ نے کی ہے یعنی کوکھ پر ہاتھ رکھنا، یہی تفسیر اولی اور انسب ہے۔ بعض نے کہا کہ تخصر عصا پر ٹیک لگانا ہے۔ اور بعض نے کہا کہ تخصر یہ ہے کہ آیت سجدہ کو حذف کر دے اور باقی کو پڑھے۔
And he should not place his hands on his hips (takhassur) because he صلى الله عليه وسلم forbade placing hands on hips in prayer, and because it involves abandoning the sunnah posture. Explanation: Placing hands on hips during prayer is makruh tahrimi, because the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم forbade it; Abu Hurairah narrated: 'He صلى الله عليه وسلم forbade placing hands on hips in prayer.' Rational evidence: It necessitates abandoning the sunnah posture. Outside of prayer, it is makruh tanzih for both men and women. One interpretation of takhassur by the author of al-Hidayah is placing hands on the hips; this is the primary and most appropriate interpretation. Some say it means leaning on a staff, and some say it means omitting the verse of prostration and reciting the rest.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage rules on takhassur (تخصر), which is commonly understood as placing one's hands on the hips (akhtasar) during prayer. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in prayer, based on a sound hadith from Abu Hurairah forbidding it. The rational argument is that it violates the sunnah posture (sunnah position of hands). Outside of prayer, it is merely makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) for both genders. The text then discusses different interpretations of the term 'takhassur': the preferred view is placing hands on the hips; other views include leaning on a staff or omitting a sajdah verse from recitation. The first interpretation is considered most appropriate.

Key Terms
تخصر (takhassur): Placing hands on the hips with the elbows bent outward; also interpreted as leaning on a staff or omitting a sajdah verse.
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): A disliked act that is close to haram; the doer is blameworthy and it is better to avoid it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a man placing his hands on his hips during prayer in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Haram (forbidden)
B) Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked)
C) Makruh tanzih (lightly disliked)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Placing hands on hips in prayer is makruh tahrimi due to the Prophet's explicit prohibition (hadith) and the departure from the sunnah posture.

Looking Around in Prayer: The Hanafi Ruling on Iltifat
Ruling on turning the face and looking around during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iltifat (looking around) in prayer, distinguish between turning the neck (makruh) and glancing with the eyes (not makruh), and explain the supporting proofs.

Core Idea
Looking around during prayer is makruh, not invalidating—the devil snatches reward, but the prayer remains valid.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on iltifat (التفات) during salah. Based on authentic hadith and rational analogy, the school distinguishes between turning the neck (which is makruh tanzih) and merely shifting the eyes (which is not disliked). Understanding this distinction helps preserve both focus and the validity of the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what iltifat means: turning the face/neck during prayer. The hukm for turning the neck is makruh tanzih; glancing with the eye without neck movement is not disliked (la yukrah).
2
Locate the dalil
Two primary proofs are cited: the hadith 'If the worshipper knew with whom he is conversing, he would not look around' and the hadith of Aisha describing iltifat as 'a snatch (ikhtilas) by shaytan.'
The second hadith is from Bukhari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how these proofs indicate karahah. The Prophet's statement implies avoidance, not invalidation. The word 'ikhtilas' (snatching) suggests loss of reward, not the entire prayer. A rational qiyas is also provided: partial deviation from qibla is like small actions (makruh), while full deviation would invalidate.
The qiyas analogizes partial deviation to 'amal qalil' (small action) which is makruh.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider excessive iltifat as invalidating if it involves turning the whole chest. The Hanafi school holds that iltifat does not invalidate, even if it involves turning the neck.
The text explicitly concludes that iltifat is not mufsid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلتفت لقوله عليه السلام لو علم المصلى من يناجى ما التفت ولو نظر بمؤخر عينيه يمنة ويسرة من غير ان يلوى عنقه لا يكره لانه عليه السلام كان يلاحظ اصحابه في صلاته بمؤخر عينيه. تشریح: مسئلہ گردن موڑ کر التفات نہ کرے کیونکہ اس میں کراہت ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا کہ اگر مصلی جانتا کہ کس سے مناجات کرتا ہے تو التفات نہ کرتا۔ نیز حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے کہ ان الرحمته تواجه العبد مادام في صلاته فاذا التفت اعرض عنه یعنی اللہ تعالی برابر بندہ پر نماز میں اقبال فرماتا ہے پس جب اس نے التفاف کیا تو وہ وجہ کریم اس سے پھیر لیتا ہے۔ عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ گردن موڑ کر التفات کرنے میں بعض بدن کے ساتھ انحراف عن القبلہ ہے اگر پورے بدن کے ساتھ انحراف عن القبلہ ہوتا تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہو جاتی۔ پس جب بعض بدن کے ساتھ انحراف عن القبلہ ہوا تو نماز مکروہ ہوگی۔ جیسے نماز کے اندر عمل قلیل کرہ ہے کیونکہ عمل کثیر مفسد صلوۃ ہے۔ حضرت عائشہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہا فرماتی ہیں سالت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن التفات الرجل في الصلاة فقال هو اختلاس يختلسه الشيطان من صلاة العبد یعنی میں نے رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مرد کی نماز میں التفات کے متعلق دریافت کیا تو آپ نے فرمایا کہ وہ اختلاس ہے کہ اس کو بندہ کی نماز میں سے شیطان اچک لیتا ہے۔ (بخاری) بہر حال ان روایات اور عقلی دلیل سے یہ بات ثابت ہوگئی کہ التفات مفسد نماز نہیں اگر چہ دائیں یا بائیں جانب انحراف عن القبلہ ہو۔
And he should not look around because of his saying عليه السلام: If the worshipper knew with whom he is conversing, he would not look around. And if he looks with the corner of his eye to the right and left without twisting his neck, it is not disliked, because he عليه السلام used to observe his companions during his prayer with the corner of his eye. Explanation: The ruling is that one should not turn the neck to look around because there is karahah in it. The evidence is that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said: If the worshipper knew with whom he is conversing, he would not look around. Also narrated from the Prophet: 'Indeed mercy faces the servant as long as he is in prayer; when he turns away, it turns away from him.' Rational evidence: Turning the neck involves partial deviation from the qibla; if the entire body deviated, the prayer would be invalid. So when only part of the body deviates, the prayer becomes makruh, just as a small amount of action in prayer is makruh, while a large amount invalidates it. Aisha may Allah be pleased with her said: I asked the Messenger of Allah about a man's looking around in prayer, and he said: 'It is a snatch that the devil snatches from the servant's prayer.' (Bukhari) In any case, these narrations and rational evidence establish that looking around does not invalidate the prayer, even if it involves turning to the right or left away from the qibla.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on iltifat (التفات) which means looking around during prayer. The general ruling is that it is makruh to turn the neck to look around. However, glancing with the corner of the eye without turning the neck is not disliked, as the Prophet himself did this. Several proofs are given: a hadith stating that the worshipper would not look around if he knew he was conversing with Allah; a hadith about mercy turning away when one looks around; a rational analogy that partial deviation from qibla is like small actions (makruh) while full deviation would invalidate. The hadith of Aishah describes iltifat as a snatch by shaytan. Despite all this, the text concludes that iltifat does not invalidate the prayer, even if it involves turning away from qibla; it only renders the prayer makruh. This is the Hanafi position: iltifat is makruh, not mufsid. The distinction between turning the neck (makruh) and glancing with the eyes (not makruh) is important.

Key Terms
التفات (iltifat): Turning the face or looking around during prayer, especially with the neck.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): A snatch or grab; here it refers to the devil stealing parts of the prayer's reward through iltifat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a person who looks to the right during prayer using only his eyes (without turning his neck)?
A) It invalidates the prayer
B) It is makruh
C) It is not disliked (لا يكره)
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
Iltifat is makruh tanzih, not invalidating; the worshipper should strive for full focus, but accidental or even deliberate glances with neck movement do not break the prayer.

Rulings on Looking, Iqa', and Iftirash in Prayer
Rulings on looking, iqa', and iftirash with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking around, iqa', and iftirash in prayer, and explain the proof from hadith, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of iqa'.

Core Idea
Looking around in prayer is allowed, but raising gaze, iqa', and spreading arms in sujud are makruh.

This passage from 'Ashraf al-Hidayah' addresses three actions that are disliked (makruh) in prayer: raising the gaze to the sky, sitting like a dog (iqa'), and spreading the forearms in prostration (iftirash). It also clarifies that looking from the corner of the eye without turning the neck is not disliked, based on the Prophet's practice. The proof comes from hadith, and the passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Tahawi and Imam Karakhi on the precise definition of iqa'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disliked actions
List the three actions explicitly ruled as makruh: raising gaze to the sky, iqa' (sitting like a dog), and iftirash (spreading forearms in sujud). Also note that looking from the corner of the eye is not disliked.
The hadith mentions pecking like a rooster (naqrat al-deek), but the passage focuses on iqa' and iftirash.
2
Understand the hukm level
These actions are makruh tanzihi (disliked, not forbidden). The proof is that the Prophet prohibited them, but prohibition in non-obligatory matters indicates karahah, not tahrim.
Some scholars might consider them makruh tahrimi, but the Hanafi position is tanzihi.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith of Abu Dharr and Abu Huraira are cited. In the hadith, the Prophet forbade (نهى) these actions. The command of prohibition (nahy) indicates the action is makruh.
The passage notes that the version from Abu Huraira is more authentic.
4
Analyze wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (نهى) is used, which in Hanafi usul indicates that the action is makruh unless there is evidence for tahrim. Since the actions are not major sins, they are makruh tanzihi.
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that al-nahy yufidu al-tahrim illa bi-qarinah.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on iqa'
Imam Tahawi defines iqa' as sitting on the buttocks with thighs erect and knees to chest, hands on ground. Imam Karakhi defines it as standing on the feet with hands on ground. The author of al-Hidayah prefers Tahawi's definition.
The practical difference is which posture is considered makruh. Both agree iqa' is disliked.

If the worshipper looks from the corner of his eye to the right or left without turning his neck, it is not disliked, because the Prophet used to observe his companions from the corner of his eye in prayer. However, raising the gaze to the sky is disliked. Sitting like a dog and spreading the forearms on the ground are also disliked. He should not sit in iqa' nor spread his forearms, due to the saying of Abu Dharr: 'My intimate friend prohibited me from three things: pecking like a rooster, sitting in iqa' like a dog, and spreading like a fox.' Iqa' is to place the buttocks on the ground and erect the knees. Translation: And iqa' (sitting like a dog) should not be done, nor should he spread his forearms, because Abu Dharr said: 'My intimate friend forbade me three things: (one) pecking like a rooster, (two) sitting in iqa' like a dog, (three) spreading forearms like a fox.' That is to place his two buttocks on the ground and erect his two knees. That is correct. Commentary: Sahib al-Quduri said that iqa' and spreading the two hands in prostration are disliked. Sahib al-Hidayah presented the proof of Abu Dharr, but the commentators of al-Hidayah said that Imam Ahmad narrated in his Musnad the saying of Abu Huraira, and the saying of Abu Dharr is rare. The text of the hadith is: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade me from three things: pecking like a rooster, sitting like a dog, and looking around like a fox.' Abu Huraira said: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade me from three things: one, pecking like a rooster, i.e., prostrating so quickly like a rooster pecks and raises its head; two, sitting like a dog i.e., sitting like a dog between the tashahhud and the two prostrations; three, looking around like a fox.' And in the hadith of Abu Dharr mentioned by Sahib al-Hidayah, the third thing is 'spreading forearms like a fox,' i.e., he forbade spreading the hands in prostration. Forms of iqa': Two interpretations of iqa' have been given: one according to Imam Tahawi and the other according to Imam Karakhi. According to Imam Tahawi, iqa' is to sit on the buttocks, erect the thighs, bring the knees close to the chest, and place the hands on the ground. That is the interpretation he chose. According to Imam Karakhi, iqa' is to erect both feet and place both hands on the ground.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from 'Ashraf al-Hidayah' deals with three disliked actions in prayer. First, looking around with the eye without turning the neck is not disliked because the Prophet himself did so, but looking up at the sky is disliked. Second, sitting like a dog (iqa') is disliked. The text provides two definitions of iqa' from Hanafi scholars: Imam Tahawi's definition (sitting on buttocks with thighs erect and knees to chest) and Imam Karakhi's definition (erecting feet and placing hands on ground). The author of al-Hidayah preferred Tahawi's view. Third, prostrating with arms spread like a fox is also disliked. The proof comes from a hadith of Abu Dharr, though some commentators note that the more authentic version is from Abu Huraira which mentions pecking like a rooster (quick prostration), sitting like a dog (between tashahhud and sujood), and looking around like a fox. The hadith of Abu Dharr adds spreading arms like a fox in prostration. The main point is that these actions are makruh (disliked) in prayer, and the worshipper should avoid them.

Key Terms
اقعاء (iqa'): A sitting posture in prayer where the buttocks are on the ground and knees are erect, similar to a dog. Two definitions exist within the Hanafi school.
افتراش الذراعين (iftirash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms on the ground in prostration, like a fox or lion. Disliked in prayer.
نقرة الديك (naqrat al-deek): Pecking like a rooster, i.e., doing sajdah so quickly that one barely touches the ground and immediately rises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Looking from the corner of the eye without turning the neck
C) Sitting like a dog (iqa')
D) Prostrating with arms spread like a fox

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, looking at the sky, iqa', and spreading forearms in sujud are makruh tanzihi, but glancing from the corner of the eye is permissible.

Responding to Salam in Prayer
Ruling on responding to salam in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on responding to salam in prayer and distinguish between verbal response, hand gesture, and handshake with intention.

Core Idea
Verbal salam-response invalidates prayer; hand gesture is disliked; handshake with intention invalidates.

A person praying may be greeted by someone. How should the one in prayer respond? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the type of response and intention. This lesson unpacks the legal categories of kalaam (speech), gesture, and handshake, and addresses an objection from a hadith of the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'responding to salam while in prayer'. Determine the hukm level for each type of response: verbal, hand gesture, and handshake with intention.
The matn states: 'Do not respond with tongue... not with hand... even if he shakes hands with intention, it invalidates.'
2
Locate the dalil
The author provides a secondary dalil from an oath-breaking scenario to prove that salam is speech. Also, the opposing dalil (hadith of Bilal) is addressed.
The commentator uses qiyas: salam is speech like other speech, so it invalidates prayer.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For verbal response: it is kalaam (speech) which invalidates prayer. For hand gesture: it is 'salam in meaning' so it is at least makruh. For handshake with intention: it becomes an act of greeting performed with intention, invalidating prayer.
The Hanafi position on hand gesture being makruh is based on it being a form of greeting, not on it being kalaam.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and its response
The Shafi'i school uses the hadith of Bilal to permit hand gestures. The Hanafi response is that this hadith is abrogated or refers to indicating one is in prayer, not returning the salam.
This is a common inter-madhhab dispute.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position (verbal invalidates, hand gesture makruh) is mufta bihi. The opposing view is considered weak due to abrogation.
The authority weight is zahir_riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نماز میں سلام کا جواب دینے کا حکم
ولا يرد السلام بلسانه لانه كلام ولا بيده لانه سلام معنى حتى لوصافح بنية التسليم تفسد
ترجمہ اور اپنی زبان سے سلام کا جواب نہ دے کیونکہ یہ کلام سے اور نہ اپنے ہاتھ سے کیونکہ معنی یہ بھی سلام ہے حتی کہ اگر وہ سے مصافحہ کیا تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
باب ما يفسد الصلوة وما يكره فيها
تشریح نماز میں زبان سے سلام کا جواب دینا مفسد نماز ہے کیونکہ یہ کلام ہے اور کام نماز کو فاسد کر دیتا ہے لہذا اسلام کا جواب بھی نماز کو فاسد کر دے گا۔ سلام اور جواب سلام کے کلام ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں فلاں سے کام نہیں کروں گا پھر اس کو سلام کیا تو ہی شخص حانث ہو جائے گا اور ہاتھ سے سلام کا جواب دینا مکروہ ہے کیونکہ یہ بھی معنی سلام ہے چنانچہ بہ بیت سلام اگر مصافحہ کیا تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
یہاں ایک اعتراض ہے وہ یہ کہ حضرت ابن عمر رضی اللہ عنہما نے کہا کہ میں نے بادل سے کہا کہ كيف كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يرد عليهم حين كانوا يسلمون عليه وهو فى الصلوة قال كان يشير بيده يعنى جس وقت حضور سے نماز میں ہوتے اور لوگ آپ کو سلام کرتے تو آپ کس طرح جواب دیتے تھے بلال نے کہا کہ ہاتھ سے اشارہ فرماتے تھے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ ہاتھ سے سلام کا جواب دینا مکروہ نہیں ہے۔ جواب یہ واقعہ ماقبل التحریم پرمحمول ہے لہذا اس کو عدم کراہت کی دلیل نہ بنایا جائے۔
Chapter: The ruling on responding to salam in prayer. Do not respond to salam with his tongue because it is speech, and not with his hand because it is salam in meaning, even if he shakes hands with the intention of salam, it invalidates. Translation: Do not respond to salam with your tongue because it is speech, and not with your hand because it is also salam in meaning, so much so that if he shakes hands with the intention of salam, his prayer becomes invalid. Chapter: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. Commentary: Responding to salam with the tongue in prayer invalidates prayer because it is speech, and speech invalidates prayer. Therefore, responding to the greeting also invalidates prayer. The evidence that salam and its response are speech is that if someone swears an oath that he will not speak to a certain person, then he greets him, he becomes a breaker of the oath. And responding with the hand is disliked because it is also salam in meaning. Therefore, if he shakes hands with the intention of salam, his prayer becomes invalid. There is an objection: Ibn Umar said that he asked Bilal: 'How did the Prophet respond when people greeted him while he was in prayer?' Bilal said: 'He used to gesture with his hand.' This indicates that responding with the hand is not disliked. The answer is that this incident occurred before the prohibition, so it cannot be taken as proof for permissibility.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of responding to salaam (greeting) while in prayer. The matn states that one should not respond with the tongue because it is kalaam (speech), which invalidates prayer. Nor should one respond with the hand because that too is a form of greeting (salaam in meaning). If one goes further and shakes hands with the intention of giving salaam, the prayer becomes invalid (fāsid). The commentary clarifies that verbal response invalidates because it is speech. An objection is raised: the Prophet gestured with his hand in prayer to respond to salaam, as narrated from Bilal. The response is that this practice was before the prohibition of speech in prayer; or it was for indicating that one is in prayer, not for returning the greeting. Therefore, the ruling remains: verbal response invalidates, hand gesture is disliked but does not invalidate unless done with the intention of returning the greeting (which is still according to the relied-upon position).

Key Terms
كلام (kalaam): Speech that invalidates prayer, including any verbal utterance of two or more letters intended for communication.
مصافحہ (musaafahah): Shaking hands. In prayer, if done with the intention of returning salam, it invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person in prayer shakes hands with the greeter intending to return the salam, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalidated.
B) The prayer is valid, but it is disliked.
C) The prayer is valid if the handshake is brief.
D) The prayer becomes invalid only if the person speaks.

Key Takeaway
Verbal response to salam invalidates prayer; hand gesture is disliked; handshake with intention invalidates—intention is key.

Sitting Cross-Legged in Prayer: Ruling and Reason
Ruling with proof and internal expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting cross-legged in prayer and explain the correct reason for its dislike, and also state the ruling on coiling hair in prayer.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is disliked because it abandons the sunnah posture, not because it resembles arrogance.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') during prayer. The matn states it is disliked unless there is an excuse, because it involves leaving the sunnah of sitting. Some scholars suggested an additional reason—arrogance—but Imam al-Sarakhsi refuted that, showing the Prophet himself sat cross-legged outside prayer. We will also cover the related ruling on coiling hair in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling in the matn
Read the matn: 'He should not sit cross-legged except due to an excuse.' This establishes the default dislike (karahah).
The ruling is makruh tanzihi, not haram.
2
Find the initial proof
The matn says the dislike is because 'in that there is leaving the sunnah of sitting.' This is the primary reason: abandoning the recommended posture.
The sunnah is to sit on the left foot with the right foot upright (iftirash) during tashahhud.
3
Note the alternative reason mentioned
Some scholars added that cross-legged sitting is the posture of the arrogant, implying it would be disliked even outside prayer.
This would extend the ruling beyond prayer.
4
Analyze Sarakhsi's refutation
Al-Sarakhsi refutes the arrogance reason by citing the Prophet's own practice of sitting cross-legged with his companions outside prayer, and the general practice in the mosque. Thus, the dislike is specific to prayer due to abandoning the sunnah.
The correct view is that the cause (illah) is leaving the sunnah, not arrogance.
5
Derive the correct understanding
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is disliked (makruh tanzihi) without excuse because it contradicts the sunnah posture. With an excuse (e.g., pain), it becomes permissible. Outside prayer, it is fine.
The second ruling on coiling hair: the hadith forbids it; coiling hair prevents proper prostration, so it is prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولايتربع الا من عذر لان فيه ترك سنة القعود ولا يعقص شعره وهو ان يجمع شعره على هامته ويشده بخيط او يصمغ ليتلبد فقد روى انه عليه السلام نهى ان يصلى الرجل وهو معقوص
تشریح مسئلہ نماز کی حالت میں بلا عذر چار زانو بیٹھنا مکروہ ہے کیونکہ اس بیٹھک میں قعود کی سنت کا ترک ہے بعض حضرات نے کراہت کی علت یہ بیان کی کہ متکبروں کی بیٹھک ہے پس اس ملت کی بناء پر یہ بیٹھک خارج نماز بھی مکروہ ہو گی لیکن شمس الائمہ سرخسی انیر نے اس کو دکر دیا کیونکہ خارج نماز حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کا اپنے صحابہ کے ساتھ چار زانو بیٹھنا ثابت ہے۔ (فتح القدیر ) اسی طرح مسجد نبوی میں اروق اعظم کی عام نشست تربعاً ( چار زانو) ہوتی تھی صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ چار زانو بیٹھنے کی بہ نسبت دونوں گھٹنوں پر بیٹھنا تواضع کے زیادہ اب ہے۔ لہذا نماز کی حالت میں بھی یہی بیٹھک اولی ہے الا کی کوئی عذر ہو۔
He should not sit cross-legged except due to an excuse, because in that there is leaving the sunnah of sitting. And he should not coil his hair, i.e., gather his hair on his head and tie it with thread or gum to make it matted, for it is narrated that the Prophet forbade a man from praying while his hair is coiled. Commentary: It is disliked to sit cross-legged in prayer without an excuse because this sitting abandons the sunnah of sitting (sitting on the feet). Some scholars stated the cause of dislike as that it is the sitting of the arrogant, so based on this, this sitting would be disliked even outside prayer. But Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi refuted this because outside prayer, the Prophet's sitting cross-legged with his companions is established. Similarly, in the Prophet's mosque, the general sitting of the great imam (?) was cross-legged. The correct view is that sitting on the knees is more indicative of humility than sitting cross-legged. Therefore, in prayer, this sitting is better unless there is an excuse.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage covers two separate rulings: sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') and coiling hair ('aqs). Regarding sitting cross-legged, the matn states that it should not be done except for an excuse because it involves abandoning the sunnah of sitting (which in prayer is sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright during tashahhud, or sitting on the knees when resting). The commentary mentions an additional reason: some scholars said it resembles the sitting of the arrogant, but Imam al-Sarakhsi refutes that because the Prophet himself sat cross-legged outside prayer. Therefore, the dislike is specific to prayer, not outside. The preferred sitting in prayer for tashahhud is the sunnah position, not cross-legged, unless one has an excuse such as pain or difficulty.

Key Terms
تربع (tarbbu'): Sitting cross-legged in prayer, which is disliked when there is no excuse.
عقص ('aqs): Coiling or matting the hair on the head before prayer, forbidden because it prevents proper prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is only disliked if done for show.

Key Takeaway
Sitting cross-legged in prayer without excuse is disliked (makruh tanzihi) because it abandons the sunnah posture; with excuse, it is permissible.

Ruling on Coiling Hair (عقص) in Prayer
Ruling on coiling hair during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coiling hair during prayer and identify the dalil from hadith that supports it.

Core Idea
Coiling hair in prayer is makruh because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the body.

When performing salat, Muslims are encouraged to humble themselves fully before Allah. This includes allowing all parts of the body, including the hair, to prostrate naturally. The Hanafi school rules that coiling hair ('aqs) on the head — whether by braiding, tying with thread, or using adhesive — is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) during prayer. This lesson explores the definition of 'aqs, the evidence from the Prophet's (ﷺ) prohibitions, and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Define 'aqs
Understand the act of coiling hair: gathering hair on the crown and tying or gumming it to become matted.
Three forms: braiding, tying with thread, using adhesive.
2
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi hukm: it is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked).
Not haram because the evidence is ahad (zanni).
3
Locate the primary dalil
Recall the hadith of Abu Rafi': the Prophet forbade a man from praying while his hair is coiled.
The word 'نهى' indicates prohibition, but due to zanni thubut, it yields karahah.
4
Examine the secondary dalil
The hadith commanding prostration on seven parts and not folding hair or garment supports the natural prostration of hair.
This hadith is also zanni.
5
Understand the 'illah
The effective cause: coiling prevents the hair from spreading naturally during sujud.
Also resembles practices of non-Muslims or women, but the primary reason is the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For it is narrated that the Prophet forbade a man from praying while his hair is coiled. Commentary: It is also disliked to coil the hair of the head in prayer. Sahib al-Kifayah wrote three forms of coiling hair: (1) Making braids around the head and tying them like women do. (2) Gathering at the forehead and tying with thread. (3) using sticky stuff or gum to paste it. Proof: Hadith of Abu Rafi' who said: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade a man from praying while his hair is coiled.' Meaning the Prophet prohibited a man from praying in such a state. Also narrated from the Prophet: 'I am commanded to prostrate on seven body parts and not to fold back my hair or my garment.' Meaning I am commanded to prostrate on seven parts and not to fold back hair or clothing.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on coiling hair ('aqs) during prayer. 'Aqs means to gather the hair on top of the head and tie it with thread or gum so it becomes matted. This is disliked (makruh) in prayer. The commentary by Sahib al-Kifayah details three forms: braiding around the head like women, gathering at the forehead and tying, and using adhesive. The proof is a hadith of Abu Rafi' that the Prophet forbade a man from praying while his hair is coiled. Another hadith mentions that the Prophet was commanded not to fold back his hair or clothing during prostration. The underlying reason is that coiling hair prevents the hair from naturally prostrating along with the body, and it resembles the practice of non-Muslims or women, so it is discouraged.

Key Terms
عقص (aqs): Coiling the hair on the head, either by braiding, tying, or gumming it together.

Coiling hair (عقص) in prayer is makruh tanzih because the Prophet forbade it and commanded not to fold hair during sujud.

Responding to Salaam in Prayer: Words Invalidate, Gesture is Makruh
Rules for responding to salaam in prayer with words and gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on responding to salaam during prayer and explain why the hadith of Bilal is not evidence for permissibility.

Core Idea
Verbal response in prayer invalidates; gesture is makruh; the Prophet's gesture was before the prohibition.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on returning greetings during prayer. The text distinguishes between speech, which invalidates the prayer, and gestures, which are merely disliked. An apparent objection from a hadith is resolved by noting that it occurred before the prohibition of speaking in prayer, thus it is abrogated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the two acts: responding to salaam with words, and responding with a gesture. State the Hanafi ruling for each.
Verbal response invalidates; gesture is makruh.
2
Locate the dalil for verbal invalidation
The proof is qiyas: if someone swears not to speak to a person, greeting them breaks the oath, proving that greeting is speech (kalam).
This qiyas establishes that salaam is a form of speech.
3
Analyze the objection from the hadith of Bilal
The hadith shows the Prophet gesturing in response to salaam. The objector argues this makes gesture permissible.
The hadith is zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the hadith is abrogated; it occurred before the prohibition of speaking in prayer. Therefore, it does not prove permissibility after prohibition.
Abrogation (naskh) is a key usul principle here.
5
Determine the practical ruling
Thus, the mufta bihi ruling is: verbal response invalidates prayer; gesture is makruh tanzih and does not invalidate.
The ruling applies to all forms of verbal greeting responses in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلوة وما يكره فيها
تشریح نماز میں زبان سے سلام کا جواب دینا مفسد نماز ہے کیونکہ یہ کلام ہے اور کام نماز کو فاسد کر دیتا ہے لہذا اسلام کا جواب بھی نماز کو فاسد کر دے گا۔ سلام اور جواب سلام کے کلام ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ میں فلاں سے کام نہیں کروں گا پھر اس کو سلام کیا تو ہی شخص حانث ہو جائے گا اور ہاتھ سے سلام کا جواب دینا مکروہ ہے کیونکہ یہ بھی معنی سلام ہے چنانچہ بہ بیت سلام اگر مصافحہ کیا تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
یہاں ایک اعتراض ہے وہ یہ کہ حضرت ابن عمر رضی اللہ عنہما نے کہا کہ میں نے بادل سے کہا کہ كيف كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يرد عليهم حين كانوا يسلمون عليه وهو فى الصلوة قال كان يشير بيده يعنى جس وقت حضور سے نماز میں ہوتے اور لوگ آپ کو سلام کرتے تو آپ کس طرح جواب دیتے تھے بلال نے کہا کہ ہاتھ سے اشارہ فرماتے تھے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ ہاتھ سے سلام کا جواب دینا مکروہ نہیں ہے۔ جواب یہ واقعہ ماقبل التحریم پرمحمول ہے لہذا اس کو عدم کراہت کی دلیل نہ بنایا جائے۔
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. Explanation: Responding to salam with the tongue during prayer invalidates the prayer because it is speech, and speech invalidates prayer. Therefore, responding to salam will also invalidate prayer. The proof that salam and its response are speech is that if someone swore an oath saying 'I will not speak to so-and-so', then he greeted him, that person would become a sinner. And responding to salam with the hand is makruh because it also signifies salam. Thus, if he shakes hands in the salam, his prayer invalidates. Here there is an objection: Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said that he asked Bilal: 'How did the Prophet (peace be upon him) respond to them when they greeted him while he was in prayer?' Bilal said: 'He would gesture with his hand.' This indicates that responding to salam with the hand is not makruh. The answer: This incident is carried on before the prohibition (of speaking in prayer), so it should not be taken as a proof for non-dislikeness.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on prayer explains the ruling on responding to greetings (salam) while in prayer. The Hanafi position is clear: any verbal response, including returning a salam, invalidates the prayer because it falls under the category of human speech (kalam), which is impermissible in prayer. The proof is analogical: if salam and its response were not speech, then breaking an oath by speaking would not occur when one greets another. However, responding with a gesture (like a hand wave) is merely makruh (disliked) and does not invalidate the prayer. The objection from the hadith of Bilal, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) gestured in response to salam, is answered by noting that this was before the prohibition of speaking in prayer (i.e., abrogated). Therefore, the ruling remains that gestures are disliked, not permissible.

Key Terms
کلام (kalam): Speech; any deliberate utterance of human words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Rulings on Sitting Cross-Legged and Plaiting Hair in Prayer
Explanation of the dislike of tarbi' and 'aqs during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tarbi' and 'aqs in prayer, explain the underlying proofs, and apply the ruling to scenarios with or without excuses.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged and plaiting hair in prayer are disliked (makruh) due to abandoning sunna and explicit prohibition.

In this lesson, we examine two specific actions in prayer: sitting cross-legged (tarbi') and plaiting the hair ('aqs). The Hanafi school considers both makruh tanzih, meaning they are discouraged but not invalidating. We will explore the textual proofs and reasoning behind these rulings, including a useful intra-Hanafi discussion on the scope of the dislike for sitting cross-legged outside prayer. By the end, you will be able to identify when these actions are permissible due to excuse and when they remain disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions and their ruling
The passage explicitly rules on sitting cross-legged (tarbi') and plaiting hair ('aqs) during prayer. State that both are makruh tanzih unless there is an excuse.
2
Understand the reason for dislike of tarbi'
The matn says it abandons the sunna of sitting (qa'd). The commentary adds that some considered it the sitting of the arrogant, but al-Sarakhsi refuted that extension. Focus on the core reason: leaving the sunna posture.
The sunna sitting is iftirash (sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright) in most positions, or tawarruk (sitting on the left hip) in the final tashahhud.
3
Understand the reason for dislike of 'aqs
The prohibition is from a hadith: the Prophet forbade praying with plaited hair. The commentary explains that 'aqs prevents the hair from prostrating with the worshipper, contradicting the command to prostrate on all limbs.
The hadith is also linked to the general principle: do not fold back hair or garment during prostration.
4
Apply the exception clause
Both rulings include an exception: 'except due to an excuse'. For tarbi', an excuse could be a knee injury; for 'aqs, it could be preventing pain or excessive hair that distracts. When an excuse exists, the action is no longer makruh.
The excuse must be genuine and not a habitual choice.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance on tarbi' outside prayer
Some scholars argued that tarbi' is also makruh outside prayer because it resembles arrogance, but al-Sarakhsi rejected this because the Prophet himself sat cross-legged with companions. Thus, the ruling is specific to prayer.
This is a good example of how a general cause (arrogance) can be overridden by specific evidence (prophetic practice).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولايتربع الا من عذر لان فيه ترك سنة القعود ولا يعقص شعره وهو ان يجمع شعره على هامته ويشده بخيط او يصمغ ليتلبد فقد روى انه عليه السلام نهى ان يصلى الرجل وهو معقوص ترجمہ اور چار زانو نہ بیٹھے مگر عذر کی وجہ سے کیونکہ اس میں سنت قعود کا ترک ہے اور بالوں کو معقوص نہ کرے۔ اور عقص یہ ہے کہ اپنے بالوں کو پیشانی پر جمع کر کے دھاگے سے باندھے یا گوند سے چوڑا کر دے تاکہ چپک جائے کیونکہ مروی ہے کہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے معقوص ہونے کی حالت میں نماز پڑھنے سے منع فرمایا۔
تشریح مسئلہ نماز کی حالت میں بلا عذر چار زانو بیٹھنا مکروہ ہے کیونکہ اس بیٹھک میں قعود کی سنت کا ترک ہے بعض حضرات نے کراہت کی علت یہ بیان کی کہ متکبروں کی بیٹھک ہے پس اس ملت کی بناء پر یہ بیٹھک خارج نماز بھی مکروہ ہو گی لیکن شمس الائمہ سرخسی انیر نے اس کو دکر دیا کیونکہ خارج نماز حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کا اپنے صحابہ کے ساتھ چار زانو بیٹھنا ثابت ہے۔ (فتح القدیر ) اسی طرح مسجد نبوی میں اروق اعظم کی عام نشست تربعاً ( چار زانو) ہوتی تھی صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ چار زانو بیٹھنے کی بہ نسبت دونوں گھٹنوں پر بیٹھنا تواضع کے زیادہ اب ہے۔ لہذا نماز کی حالت میں بھی یہی بیٹھک اولی ہے الا کی کوئی عذر ہو۔
نماز کی حالت میں سر کے بالوں کو چلا بنانا بھی مکروہ ہے۔ صاحب کفایہ نے بالوں کو عقوص کرنے کی تین صورتیں لکھی ہیں ، (1) سر کے ارد گرد بالوں کی مینڈھیاں بنا کر باندھے جیسے عورتیں کرتی ہیں۔ (۲) پیشانی پر جمع کر کے دھاگے سے باند ھے۔ کی لیس دار چیز یا گوند ہے چپکا ہے۔
دلیل ابورافع کی حدیث ہے قال نھی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ان يصلى الرجل ورأسه معقوص العنی حضور نے مرد کو اس حال میں نماز پڑھنے سے منع کیا کہ اس کے سر پر بالوں کا چلا ہو نیز حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم سے مروی ہے امرت ان اسجد علی سبعته وان لا اكف شعر اولا ثوبا ، یعنی مجھ کو سات اعضاء پر سجدہ کرنے کا حکم کیا گیا اور اس بات کا کہ بالوں کو کف نہ کروں اور نہ
The ruling on sitting cross-legged and plaiting hair in prayer. 'And he should not sit cross-legged except due to an excuse, because it involves abandoning the sunna of sitting (qa'd), and he should not plait his hair.' And 'aqs (plaiting) is to gather his hair on his forehead and tie it with a thread or glue it to make it matted. For it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a man from praying while his hair is plaited. Explanation: The ruling is that sitting cross-legged without an excuse is makruh because it abandons the sunna of sitting. Some scholars have stated the cause of dislike is that it is the sitting of the arrogant, and based on this view, such sitting outside of prayer would also be makruh. However, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi refuted this because outside of prayer, the Prophet (peace be upon him) sitting cross-legged with his companions is established. (Fath al-Qadir). Similarly, in the Prophet's mosque, the general sitting of the great leaders was cross-legged. The correct view is that sitting on the knees is more indicative of humility than sitting cross-legged. Therefore, even in prayer, this sitting is preferable unless there is an excuse. Plaiting the hair of the head during prayer is also makruh. The author of Kifaya wrote three forms of 'aqs: (1) Making plaits around the head like women do, (2) Gathering on the forehead and tying with thread, (3) Using a sticky substance or glue to make it matted. Proof: The hadith of Abu Rafi' who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade a man from praying with his head plaited. Also, it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold back hair or garment.' That is, I have been commanded to prostrate on seven limbs and not to fold hair or garment.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This section explains two disliked actions in prayer: sitting cross-legged (tarbi') and plaiting the hair ('aqs). The matn explicitly states that these are disliked unless there is an excuse. The commentator clarifies that sitting cross-legged abandons the sunna of qa'd (sitting during tashahhud). Some argued it's makruh because it resembles the arrogant, but Imam al-Sarakhsi rebutted this by noting the Prophet's own practice outside prayer. The preferred sitting is on the knees (iftirash). Plaiting hair is disliked because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the worshipper, as the hadith indicates. The ruling is consistent with the command not to 'fold back' hair or garment during prostration.

Key Terms
عقص ('aqs): Plaiting or matting hair together on the head.
تربع (tarbi'): Sitting cross-legged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on plaiting hair (عقص) during prayer in the Hanafi school?
A) It is permissible
B) It is makruh
C) It invalidates prayer
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
Sitting cross-legged and plaiting hair during prayer are disliked (makruh tanzih) but become permissible with a valid excuse.

Rulings on Kaf al-Thawb, Sadl, and Eating/Drinking in Prayer
Explanation of the dislike of kaf al-thawb, sadl, and eating/drinking in prayer with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on folding the garment (kaf al-thawb), letting it hang (sadl), and eating/drinking in prayer, and identify the exception for swallowing small particles.

Core Idea
Folding or hanging your garment in prayer shows arrogance or violates a direct prohibition — both are makruh; only swallowing small bits with saliva is excused.

In prayer, the worshipper's appearance and actions are regulated to maintain humility and focus. The Hanafi school teaches that certain habits, like adjusting or folding the garment to prevent it from touching the ground, or letting it hang loosely without method, are disliked. Even eating and drinking are prohibited, though swallowing something small stuck between teeth is excused. This lesson unpacks the proofs and nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify each act and its ruling
From the passage, list the three acts: kaf al-thawb, sadl, and eating/drinking. For each, note the ruling (makruh, prohibited, or invalidates).
Kaf al-thawb and sadl are makruh; eating/drinking is not from actions of prayer but exception exists.
2
Locate the dalil for each
For sadl, the hadith from Abu Dawood is cited. For kaf al-thawb, the reasoning of arrogance (tajabbur) is given. For eating/drinking, no specific dalil is quoted but the rationale is they are not from actions of prayer.
The hadith for sadl is explicit prohibition.
3
Understand the illa for kaf al-thawb
Kaf al-thawb is disliked because it shows concern for the garment rather than humility before Allah, thus resembling arrogance.
This is a qiyas-based ruling.
4
Analyze the exception for eating/drinking
Swallowing something stuck between teeth is excused because it is like swallowing saliva, which does not invalidate prayer. This is elaborated in the commentary.
The exception applies only if it is small and swallowed without deliberate chewing.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who after suhur has a sesame seed stuck in teeth and swallows it during prayer. Apply the ruling: valid because it's like saliva. Contrast with deliberately eating a date, which invalidates.
Test understanding of the boundary.

نماز میں کپڑے کو سمیٹنا اور سدل کرنا مکروہ ہے
ولا يكف ثوبه لانه نوع تجبر ولا يسدل ثوبه لانه عليه السلام نهى عن السدل وهو ان يجعل ثوبه على و كتفيه ثم يرسل اطرافه من جوانبه ولا يأكل ولا يشرب لانه ليس من اعمال الله ترجمہ اور اپنا کپڑا نہ سمیٹے کیونکہ اس میں ایک طرح کا تکبر ہے۔ اور نہ اپنا کپڑا لٹکائے کیونکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے لٹکانے سے منع کیا سدل یہ ہے کہ اپنا کپڑا اپنے سر اور کندھوں پر ڈال کر اس کے کنارے اپنی جوانب میں لٹکے چھوڑے اور ( نماز میں ) نہ کھائے اور کیونکہ یہ نماز کے اعمال سے نہیں ہے۔ تشریح کف ثوب یہ ہے کہ جب سجدہ کرنے کا ارادہ کرے تو اپنے آگے یا پیچھے سے کپڑا اٹھائے ۔ اب حاصل مسئلہ یہ ہوا کہ کا زمین پر گرتا ہوتو اس کو نہ رو کے کیونکہ اس میں ایک قسم کا تکبر ہے۔
اور کپڑے کو بے طریقہ لڑکا نہ چھوڑے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ امام ابو داؤد نے حضرت ابو ہریرہ سے روایت کی ہے انسہ صلی اللہ وسلم نهى عن السدل في الصلوة وان يغطى الرجل فاہ یعنی حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے نماز کے اندر سدل سے منع فرمایا اور اس سے کہ مرد اپنا منہ ڈھکے سدل یہ ہے کہ اپنا کپڑا اپنے سر اور کندھوں پر ڈال کر اس کے کنارے اپنی جوانب میں لٹکے چھوڑے۔ صاحب کفایہ نے کہا کہ سدل یہ ہے کہ چادر یا قباء اپنے کندھوں پر ڈالے اور اپنے ہاتھ کو آستیوں میں نہ ڈالے خواہ قمیص کے
قمیص کے نیچے۔ اور نماز میں نہ کھائے اور نہ پئے کیونکہ یہ نماز کے اعمال میں سے نہیں ہے لیکن اگر دانتوں کے درمیان میں کوئی چیز ہو پھر اسکار تو اس کی نماز فاسد نہ ہوگی کیونکہ جو چیز دانتوں کے درمیان ہے وہ تھوک کے تابع ہے اور تھوک کا نگل جانا مفسد نماز نہیں لہذا اس کے نگل جانا بھی مفسد نماز نہیں ہو گا۔
Folding clothes and letting them hang (sadl) in prayer is makruh. 'And he should not fold his garment because it is a type of arrogance, and he should not let his garment hang because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade sadl.' Sadl is to place the garment on the head and shoulders and then let its ends hang from the sides. And he should not eat or drink because these are not from the actions of prayer. Explanation: Kaf al-thawb (folding) is to lift the garment from the front or back when intending to prostrate. Now the gist of the matter is that if the garment touches the ground, he should not prevent it because it involves a kind of arrogance. And he should not leave the garment hanging without method. The proof is that Imam Abu Dawood narrated from Abu Hurairah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade sadl in prayer and that a man should cover his mouth. Sadl is to place the garment on the head and shoulders and let its ends hang from the sides. The author of Kifaya said that sadl is to put a sheet or cloak on the shoulders and not put his hands in the sleeves, whether it is under the shirt. And in prayer he should not eat or drink because these are not among the actions of prayer. But if there is something between the teeth and he swallows it, his prayer is not invalidated because what is between the teeth is subject to saliva, and swallowing saliva does not invalidate prayer, so swallowing it also does not invalidate prayer.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This lesson addresses three interrelated issues: folding the garment (kaf al-thawb) to prevent it from touching the ground, letting the garment hang loosely (sadl), and eating/drinking in prayer. The matn states that folding the garment is makruh because it resembles arrogance (tajabbur). Similarly, sadl is makruh because the Prophet explicitly forbade it. The commentary explains the forms of sadl and adds that eating and drinking are not from the actions of prayer. However, an exception is made for swallowing something stuck between the teeth, as it is considered an extension of swallowing saliva, which is permissible.

Key Terms
سدل (sadl): To place a garment on the head and shoulders and let the ends hang without tucking in.
كف الثوب (kaf al-thawb): Folding or gathering the garment to prevent it from touching the ground during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on sadl (letting garment hang loosely) in prayer?
A) Permissible
B) Makruh
C) Invalidates prayer
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
Folding or hanging the garment in prayer is disliked due to arrogance or explicit prohibition; eating and drinking are generally prohibited unless negligible.

Eating and Drinking Invalidates Prayer: The Kathir al-'Amal Principle
Ruling with proof: eating and drinking invalidate prayer, with evidence from kathir al-'amal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on eating or drinking during prayer in the Hanafi school and identify why it invalidates prayer based on the concept of kathir al-'amal.

Core Idea
Any eating or drinking during prayer, intentionally or forgetfully, invalidates it because it is kathir al-'amal.

This lesson addresses a critical invalidation of prayer: eating and drinking. The Hanafi school holds that even a small amount, whether done intentionally or forgetfully, nullifies the prayer. The underlying rationale is that such acts constitute kathir al-'amal (abundant action), which is incompatible with the state of prayer. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating or drinking during prayer. The ruling is that it invalidates (batil) the prayer.
2
Determine the scope
The ruling applies regardless of intention (intentional or forgetful), type of prayer (fard or nafl), and amount (a little or a lot).
This is a key point: forgetfulness does not excuse the invalidation.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is that eating and drinking are considered kathir al-'amal, and it is established that kathir al-'amal invalidates prayer in the Hanafi school.
The source text does not cite a specific hadith but relies on this established principle.
4
Understand the legal link
Eating and drinking are external actions that clearly indicate a departure from the state of prayer, making an observer doubt whether the person is in prayer. This matches the definition of kathir al-'amal.
5
Note the consequence
The prayer becomes invalid and must be fully repeated (qada). No compensation (like sajda sahw) is sufficient.
This is unlike omission of a wajib, which can be compensated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نماز میں جان بوجھ کر یا بھول کر کھانا پینا مفسد صلوٰۃ ہے
فان اكل او شرب عامدا او ناسيا فسدت صلوته لانه عمل كثير و حالة الصلوة ما ترجمہ پھر اگر نمازی نے کھایا یا پیا محمد ایا سہو سے تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہوگئی کیونکہ یہ عمل کثیر ہے اور نماز کی حالت یاد دلانے والی ہے تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ نماز کی حالت میں کھانایا پینا مفسد نماز ہے نماز خواہ فرض ہو یا نفل اور کھانا پینا عم ہو یا ہو ایا نسا نا ہو دیل کہ اکل اور شرب ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک عمل کثیر ہے اور عمل کثیر مفسد نماز ہے اس لئے ان صورتوں میں نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
Eating and drinking in prayer intentionally or forgetfully invalidates it. 'Then if he eats or drinks, intentionally or forgetfully, his prayer becomes invalid because it is a lot of action, and the state of prayer is (for remembrance).' Translation: Then if the worshipper eats or drinks, intentionally or forgetfully, his prayer becomes invalid because it is a lot of action and the state of prayer is for remembrance. Explanation: The ruling is that eating or drinking during prayer invalidates it, whether the prayer is obligatory or supererogatory, and whether the eating or drinking is a lot or a little, intentionally or forgetfully. The proof is that eating and drinking are each a kathir al-'amal (abundant action), and abundant action invalidates prayer.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This final lesson establishes a fundamental principle: any eating or drinking during prayer, whether intentional or forgetful, invalidates the prayer. The reasoning is that eating/drinking constitutes kathir al-'amal (abundant action), which is a major invalidator of prayer. The ruling applies equally to obligatory and supererogatory prayers, and there is no distinction between a large or small amount; even a single morsel or sip invalidates the prayer. This is in contrast to actions like swallowing saliva, which are not considered eating/drinking.

Key Terms
عمل کثیر ('amal kathir): Abundant action that is not part of prayer, which invalidates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, it invalidates
B) No, only intentional invalidates
C) Only if done in fard prayer
D) It does not invalidate but is makruh

Key Takeaway
Eating or drinking during prayer, whether intentional or forgetful, invalidates it under the Hanafi school because it constitutes kathir al-'amal.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Kaf al-Thawb and Sadl
States disliked actions in prayer and their proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kaf al-thawb and sadl in prayer, identify the associated proofs, and explain why they are considered makruh tanzihi.

Core Idea
Folding or letting your garment hang in prayer is disliked because it contradicts the humility required.

In the previous lesson, we learned what invalidates prayer. Now we turn to acts that are disliked (makruh) but do not invalidate. This passage from al-Hidayah focuses on two specific actions: folding the garment (kaf al-thawb) and letting the garment hang loosely (sadl). It also addresses covering the mouth. Understanding these rulings helps maintain the proper adab of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
The passage states that kaf al-thawb (folding the garment) and sadl (letting it hang) are makruh. Also covering the mouth is makruh based on the hadith.
The ruling is makruh tanzihi, meaning it is disliked but not sinful to the level of haram.
2
Understand the reason (illa)
Kaf al-thawb is makruh because it resembles arrogance (tajabbur). Sadl is makruh because the Prophet (saw) prohibited it.
The illa for kaf is the appearance of pride; for sadl it is direct prohibition.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith narrated by Abu Hurairah in Sunan Abi Dawood states: 'The Prophet (saw) prohibited sadl in prayer and that a man covers his mouth.' The word 'nahi' indicates prohibition, which in Hanafi usul implies karahah tanzihiyyah when not absolute.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it does not reach the level of haram.
4
Note the definitional details
The passage defines sadl as placing the garment on the head and shoulders and letting the ends hang. Kaf al-thawb is defined in the commentary as lifting the garment when intending to prostrate.
Sahib al-Kifayah provides an alternative definition: placing a shawl on shoulders without inserting arms.
5
Distinguish from invalidators
These acts are makruh tanzihi, not mufsidat. They do not invalidate the prayer but reduce its perfection.
The passage also mentions eating/drinking is prohibited but swallowing a small residue is not invalidating.

Source Text
